The graph matching problem is a well-studied computational problem in a great many areas of computer science.Some examples include machine learning [CSS07], computer vision [CL12], pattern recognition [BBM05], computational biology [SXB08, VCP+11], social network analysis [KL14], and de-anonimzation [NS09].111See the surveys [LR13, CFSV04], the latter of which is titled “Thirty Years of Graph Matching in Pattern Recognition”.The graph matching problem is the task of computing, given a pair (G0,G1)subscript𝐺0subscript𝐺1(G_{0},G_{1}) of n𝑛n vertex graphs, the permutationπ∗=arg⁡minπ∈𝒮n⁡‖G0−π​(G1)‖superscript𝜋subscript𝜋subscript𝒮𝑛normsubscript𝐺0𝜋subscript𝐺1\pi^{*}=\arg\min_{\pi\in\mathcal{S}_{n}}\|G_{0}-\pi(G_{1})\|(1)where we identify the graphs with their adjacency matrices, and write π​(G1)𝜋subscript𝐺1\pi(G_{1}) for the matrix obtained by permuting the rows and columns according to π𝜋\pi (i.e., the matrix P⊤​G1​Psuperscript𝑃topsubscript𝐺1𝑃P^{\top}G_{1}P where P𝑃P is the permutation matrix corresponding to π𝜋\pi).

The graph matching problem can be thought of as a noisy (and hence harder) variant of the graph isomorphism problem.In fact, the graph matching problem is NP hard in the worst case.222If we allow weights and self-loops it is equivalent to the quadratic assignment problem [Law63, BÇPP99].O’Donnell et al. also show that graph matching is hard to approximate assuming Feige’s Random 3SAT hypothesis [OWWZ14].Hence, much of the existing work is focused on practical heuristics or specific generative models.In a 2011 paper, Pedarsani et al. [PG11] introduced the correlated Erdös-Rényi model as a case study for a de-anonymization task.This is the 𝔾⁡(n,p;γ)𝔾𝑛𝑝𝛾\operatorname{\mathbb{G}}(n,p;\gamma) model in which the pair (G0,G1)subscript𝐺0subscript𝐺1(G_{0},G_{1}) is generated as follows:333Some works also studied a more general variant where G0subscript𝐺0G_{0} and G1subscript𝐺1G_{1} use different subsampling parameters γ0,γ1subscript𝛾0subscript𝛾1\gamma_{0},\gamma_{1}.

•We sample a “base graph” B𝐵B from the Erdös-Rényi distribution 𝔾⁡(n,p)𝔾𝑛𝑝\operatorname{\mathbb{G}}(n,p).•We sample π𝜋\pi at random in 𝒮nsubscript𝒮𝑛\mathcal{S}_{n} (the set of permutations on the elements [n]delimited-[]𝑛[n]).•We let G0subscript𝐺0G_{0} be a randomly subsampled subgraph of B𝐵B obtained by including every edge of B𝐵B in G0subscript𝐺0G_{0} with probability γ𝛾\gamma independently.•We let G1subscript𝐺1G_{1} be an independently subsampled subgraph of π​(B)𝜋𝐵\pi(B) obtained by including every edge of π​(B)𝜋𝐵\pi(B) in G1subscript𝐺1G_{1} with probability γ𝛾\gamma independently.Given (G0,G1)subscript𝐺0subscript𝐺1(G_{0},G_{1}), our goal is to recover π𝜋\pi.Though initially introduced as a toy model for a specific application, the problem of recovering π𝜋\pi in 𝔾⁡(n,p;γ)𝔾𝑛𝑝𝛾\operatorname{\mathbb{G}}(n,p;\gamma) is a natural and well-motivated statistical inference problem, and it has since received a lot of attention in the information theory and statistics communities (c.f., [YG13, KL14, LFP14, KHG15, CK16, CK17, MX18]).

Below we will use 𝒟struct​(n,p;γ)subscript𝒟struct𝑛𝑝𝛾\mathcal{D}_{\text{\tiny struct}}(n,p;\gamma) (or 𝒟structsubscript𝒟struct\mathcal{D}_{\text{\tiny struct}} for short, when the parameters are clear from the context) to denote the “structured” joint distribution above on triples (G0,G1,π)subscript𝐺0subscript𝐺1𝜋(G_{0},G_{1},\pi) of pairs of graphs and a permutation π𝜋\pi such that G1subscript𝐺1G_{1} is a noisy version of π​(G0)𝜋subscript𝐺0\pi(G_{0}).One can see that the graphs G0subscript𝐺0G_{0} and G1subscript𝐺1G_{1} are individually distributed according to the Erdös-Rényi distribution 𝔾⁡(n,p​γ)𝔾𝑛𝑝𝛾\operatorname{\mathbb{G}}(n,p\gamma), but there is significant correlation between G0subscript𝐺0G_{0} and G1subscript𝐺1G_{1}.It can be shown that as long as p​γ2≫log⁡n/nmuch-greater-than𝑝superscript𝛾2𝑛𝑛p\gamma^{2}\gg\log n/n, the permutation π𝜋\pi will be the one that minimizes the right-hand side of (1), and hence it is possible to recover π𝜋\pi information theoretically.Indeed, Cullina and Kivayash [CK16, CK17] precisely characterized the parameters p,γ𝑝𝛾p,\gamma for which information theoretic recovery is possible.Specifically, they showed recovery is possible if p​γ2>log⁡n+ω​(1)n𝑝superscript𝛾2𝑛𝜔1𝑛p\gamma^{2}>\tfrac{\log n+\omega(1)}{n} and impossible when p​γ2<log⁡n−ω​(1)n𝑝superscript𝛾2𝑛𝜔1𝑛p\gamma^{2}<\tfrac{\log n-\omega(1)}{n}.

However, none of these works have given efficient algorithms.Yartseva and Grossglauser [YG13] analyzed a simple algorithm known as Percolation Graph Matching (PGM), which was used successfully by Narayanan and Shmatikov [NS09] to de-anonymize many real-world networks.(Similar algorithms were also analyzed by [KL14, KHG15, LFP14].)This algorithm starts with a ”seed set” S𝑆S of vertices in G0subscript𝐺0G_{0} that are mapped by π𝜋\pi to G1subscript𝐺1G_{1}, and for which the mapping π|Sevaluated-at𝜋𝑆\pi|_{S} is given.It propagates this information according to a simple percolation, until it recovers the original permutation.Yartseva and Grossglauser gave precise characterization of the size of the seed set required as a function of p𝑝p and γ𝛾\gamma [YG13].Specifically, in the case that γ=Ω​(1)𝛾Ω1\gamma=\Omega(1) and p=n−1+δ𝑝superscript𝑛1𝛿p=n^{-1+\delta} (where the expected degree of G0subscript𝐺0G_{0} and G1subscript𝐺1G_{1} is Θ​(nδ)Θsuperscript𝑛𝛿\Theta(n^{\delta})), the size of the seed set required is |S|=n1−δ−Θ​(δ2)𝑆superscript𝑛1𝛿Θsuperscript𝛿2|S|=n^{1-\delta-\Theta(\delta^{2})}.In the general setting when one is not given such a seed set, we would require about n|S|superscript𝑛𝑆n^{|S|} steps to obtain it by brute force, which yields an exp⁡(nΩ​(1))superscript𝑛Ω1\exp(n^{\Omega(1)}) time algorithm in this regime.Lyzinski et al. [LFF+16] also gave negative results for popular convex relaxations for graph matching on random correlated graphs.

Subsequent to our work, Mossel and Xu [MX18] obtained new algorithms for the seeded setting based on a delicate analysis of local neighborhoods.Notably, they achieve recovery at the information-theoretic threshold.Though the exact dependence of the seed set size on the average degree is complicated to state, roughly speaking whenever p​n=nδ𝑝𝑛superscript𝑛𝛿pn=n^{\delta} for 1δ∈ℕ1𝛿ℕ\frac{1}{\delta}\in\mathbb{N}, their seed set has size O​(log⁡n)𝑂𝑛O(\log n), giving quasi-polynomial time algorithms.However, when 1δ∉ℕ1𝛿ℕ\frac{1}{\delta}\not\in\mathbb{N}, the seed set size is nΩ​(1)superscript𝑛Ω1n^{\Omega(1)} with the constant in the exponent depending on the difference of 1δ1𝛿\frac{1}{\delta} and ⌊1δ⌋1𝛿\lfloor\frac{1}{\delta}\rfloor.

In this work we give quasipolynomial time algorithms for recovering the hidden permutation π𝜋\pi in the 𝔾⁡(n,p;γ)𝔾𝑛𝑝𝛾\operatorname{\mathbb{G}}(n,p;\gamma) model for every constant (and even slightly sub-constant) γ𝛾\gamma and a wide range of p𝑝p.

One can see that we obtain (nearly) efficient recovery even for sub-polynomial degrees.As discussed in Section 3, our results are more general and handle (slightly) sub-constant noise γ𝛾\gamma.See Theorem 3.2 for a precise statement of the parameters (including the o​(1)𝑜1o(1) in the minimum sparsity n​p=no​(1)𝑛𝑝superscript𝑛𝑜1np=n^{o(1)}).To the best of our knowledge, the best previously known algorithms for any p​n<n1−ϵ𝑝𝑛superscript𝑛1italic-ϵpn<n^{1-\epsilon} required subexponential (i.e., exp⁡(nΩ​(1))superscript𝑛Ω1\exp(n^{\Omega(1)})) time.

At first, the requirement that the average degree p​n𝑝𝑛pn be in a union of two disjoint intervals may seem strange.Indeed, modulo a combinatorial conjecture, our algorithm works for all values of n​p∈[Ω​(log⁡n),1)𝑛𝑝Ω𝑛1np\in[\Omega(\log n),1).In order to give this conjecture, we need the following definition; for the sake of exposition, we have pared it down.For the full requirements, see Theorem 4.1.

A proof of this conjecture would immediately extend Theorem 1.1 to every p​n∈[Ω​(log⁡n),1)𝑝𝑛Ω𝑛1pn\in[\Omega(\log n),1).In fact, our proof of Theorem 1.1 proceeds by establishing this conjecture for v=Θ​(log⁡n)𝑣Θ𝑛v=\Theta(\log n) and e∈[v+o​(v),(1+1152)​v]∪[3​v,O​(v)]𝑒𝑣𝑜𝑣11152𝑣3𝑣𝑂𝑣e\in[v+o(v),(1+\frac{1}{152})v]\cup[3v,O(v)].We find it difficult to believe that the existence of a (v,e)𝑣𝑒(v,e)-test family would be discontinuous in e𝑒e as a function of v𝑣v; however our techniques for the two regimes are different, and while we did not make a special effort to optimize the constants 11521152\frac{1}{152} or 333, it seems that completely filling in the gap requires some delicate and technical combinatorial arguments.

We also consider the potentially easier “hypothesis testing” task of distinguishing a pair of graphs (G0,G1)subscript𝐺0subscript𝐺1(G_{0},G_{1}) sampled from 𝒟struct​(n,p;γ)subscript𝒟struct𝑛𝑝𝛾\mathcal{D}_{\text{\tiny struct}}(n,p;\gamma) from a pair (G0,G1)subscript𝐺0subscript𝐺1(G_{0},G_{1}) that is drawn from the “null distribution” 𝒟null​(n,p​γ)subscript𝒟null𝑛𝑝𝛾\mathcal{D}_{\text{\tiny null}}(n,p\gamma) of two independent samples from 𝔾⁡(n,p​γ)𝔾𝑛𝑝𝛾\operatorname{\mathbb{G}}(n,p\gamma).For this problem we give a polynomial time algorithm for a range of values of p𝑝p.

See Theorem 2.2 for the full settings of parameters that we achieve for distinguishing.

In this section we illustrate our approach and techniques.For the sake of simplicity and concreteness we first focus on the following task.Given a pair of graphs (G0,G1)subscript𝐺0subscript𝐺1(G_{0},G_{1}), distinguish between the following two cases for p=n−1+δ𝑝superscript𝑛1𝛿p=n^{-1+\delta} (i.e., graphs of average degree ∼nδsimilar-toabsentsuperscript𝑛𝛿\sim n^{\delta}):

Null case:(G0,G1)subscript𝐺0subscript𝐺1(G_{0},G_{1}) are drawn from the distribution 𝒟nullsubscript𝒟null\mathcal{D}_{\text{\tiny null}} of two independent graphs from the Erdös-Rényi distribution 𝔾⁡(n,p/2)𝔾𝑛𝑝2\operatorname{\mathbb{G}}(n,p/2).Planted/structured case:(G0,G1)subscript𝐺0subscript𝐺1(G_{0},G_{1}) are drawn from the distribution 𝒟struct​(n,p;1/2)subscript𝒟struct𝑛𝑝12\mathcal{D}_{\text{\tiny struct}}(n,p;1/2). That is, we sample B𝐵B from 𝔾⁡(n,p)𝔾𝑛𝑝\operatorname{\mathbb{G}}(n,p) and a random permutation π∼𝒮nsimilar-to𝜋subscript𝒮𝑛\pi\sim\mathcal{S}_{n}, and both G0subscript𝐺0G_{0} and G1subscript𝐺1G_{1} are independently subsampled subgraphs of B𝐵B where each edge is kept with probability 1/2121/2. The labels of the vertices of G1subscript𝐺1G_{1} are additionally permuted according to π𝜋\pi.

Before we present our approach to solve this problem, we explain some of the challenges.In the 𝒟nullsubscript𝒟null\mathcal{D}_{\text{\tiny null}} case the graphs G0,G1subscript𝐺0subscript𝐺1G_{0},G_{1} are completely unrelated, and there is no permutation of the vertices so that G0subscript𝐺0G_{0} and G1subscript𝐺1G_{1} overlap on more than a p𝑝p fraction of the edges, while in the 𝒟structsubscript𝒟struct\mathcal{D}_{\text{\tiny struct}} case they are “roughly isomorphic”, in the sense that there is a permutation that will make them agree on about a quarter of their edges.Since random graphs are in fact an easy instance of the graph isomorphism problem, we could perhaps hope that known graph isomorphism algorithms will actually succeed in this “random noisy” case as well.Alas, it turns out not to be the case.

We now present some rough intuition why common graph isomorphism heuristics fail in our setting.(If you are not interested in seeing why some algorithms fail but rather only why our approach succeeds, feel free to skip ahead to Section 1.3.1.)Let’s start with one of the simplest possible heuristics for graph isomorphism: sort the vertices of G0subscript𝐺0G_{0} and G1subscript𝐺1G_{1} according to their degrees and then match them to each other.If G0subscript𝐺0G_{0} and G1subscript𝐺1G_{1} are isomorphic via some permutation π𝜋\pi then it will of course be the case that the degree of every vertex v𝑣v of G0subscript𝐺0G_{0} is equal to the degree of π​(v)𝜋𝑣\pi(v) in G1subscript𝐺1G_{1}.Generally, even in a random graph, this heuristic will not completely recover the isomorphism since we will have many ties: vertices with identical degrees.Nevertheless, this approach would map many vertices correctly, and in particular the highest degree vertex in a random graph is likely to be unique and so be mapped correctly.

However, in the noisy setting, even the highest degree vertex is unlikely to be the same in both graphs.The reason is that in a random graph of average degree ΔΔ\Delta, the degrees of all the vertices are roughly distributed as independent Poisson random variable with expectation ΔΔ\Delta.The vertex v∗superscript𝑣v^{*} with highest degree in G0subscript𝐺0G_{0} is likely to have degree which is k∗∼ln⁡nsimilar-tosuperscript𝑘𝑛k^{*}\sim\sqrt{\ln n} standard deviations higher than the mean.But since the graphs are only 1414\tfrac{1}{4}-correlated, the corresponding matched vertex w∗=π​(v∗)superscript𝑤𝜋superscript𝑣w^{*}=\pi(v^{*}) is likely to have degree which is only 14​k∗14superscript𝑘\tfrac{1}{4}k^{*} higher than the mean.It can be calculated that this means that w∗superscript𝑤w^{*} is extremely unlikely to be the highest degree vertex of G1subscript𝐺1G_{1}.In fact, we expect that about n15/16superscript𝑛1516n^{15/16} vertices will have degree larger than w∗superscript𝑤w^{*}s.

In the context of graph isomorphism algorithms, we often go beyond the degree to look at the degree profile of a vertex v𝑣v, which is the set of degrees of all the neighbors of v𝑣v.In the case that G0subscript𝐺0G_{0} and G1subscript𝐺1G_{1} are isomorphic via π𝜋\pi, the degree profiles of v𝑣v and π​(v)𝜋𝑣\pi(v) are identical.However, in the noisy case when G0subscript𝐺0G_{0} and G1subscript𝐺1G_{1} are only 1414\tfrac{1}{4}-correlated, the degree profiles of v𝑣v and π​(v)𝜋𝑣\pi(v) are quite far apart.About a quarter of the neighbors of v𝑣v and π​(v)𝜋𝑣\pi(v) will be matched, but for them the degrees are only roughly correlated, rather than equal.Moreover the other three quarters of neighbors will not be matched, and for them the degrees in both graphs will just be independent Poisson variables.

Another common heuristic for graph isomorphism is to match G0subscript𝐺0G_{0} and G1subscript𝐺1G_{1} by taking their top eigenvectors and sorting them (breaking ties using lower order eigenvectors).Once again this will fail in our case, because even if the permutation was the identity, the top eigenvector of G0subscript𝐺0G_{0} is likely to be very different from the top eigenvector of G1subscript𝐺1G_{1}.This is for similar reasons as before: the top eigenvector of G0subscript𝐺0G_{0} is the vector v0subscript𝑣0v_{0} such that the quantity v0⊤​A0​v0superscriptsubscript𝑣0topsubscript𝐴0subscript𝑣0v_{0}^{\top}A_{0}v_{0} is k∗superscript𝑘k^{*} standard deviations higher than the mean for some particular value k∗superscript𝑘k^{*}.However, it is likely that the v0⊤​A1​v0superscriptsubscript𝑣0topsubscript𝐴1subscript𝑣0v_{0}^{\top}A_{1}v_{0} will only be k∗/4superscript𝑘4k^{*}/4 or so standard deviations higher than the mean, and hence v0subscript𝑣0v_{0} will not be the top eigenvector of A1subscript𝐴1A_{1}.

One could also imagine using a different heuristic, such as cycle counts, to distinguish—in Section 2, we discuss the shortcomings of such “simple” heuristics in detail.

Now that we have appreciated the failure of the canonical graph isomorphism algorithms, we describe our approach.Our approach can be thought of as “using a flock of black swans”.Specifically, suppose that H𝐻H is an O​(1)𝑂1O(1)-sized subgraph that is a “black swan,” in the sense that it has extremely low probability μ≪1much-less-than𝜇1\mu\ll 1 of appearing as a subgraph of a random graph G0subscript𝐺0G_{0} drawn from 𝔾⁡(n,p/2)𝔾𝑛𝑝2\operatorname{\mathbb{G}}(n,p/2).777For technical reasons, for the distinguishing section we actually take μ=O​(1)𝜇𝑂1\mu=O(1) and only use μ≪1much-less-than𝜇1\mu\ll 1 for recovery, but we discuss here the case μ≪1much-less-than𝜇1\mu\ll 1 for intuition.Another way to say it is that 𝔼XH​(G)=μ𝔼subscript𝑋𝐻𝐺𝜇\operatorname*{\mathbb{E}}X_{H}(G)=\mu where XH​(G)subscript𝑋𝐻𝐺X_{H}(G) is the subgraph count of H𝐻H in G𝐺G, or the number of subgraphs of G𝐺G isomorphic to H𝐻H.888More formally, XH​(G)subscript𝑋𝐻𝐺X_{H}(G) is the number of injective homomorphisms of H𝐻H to G𝐺G, divided by the number of automorphisms of H𝐻H. That is, if H𝐻H has vertex set [v]delimited-[]𝑣[v] and G𝐺G has vertex set [n]delimited-[]𝑛[n], then XH​(G)=1|aut⁡(H)|​∑σ:[v]→[n]​1-to-1∏(i,j)∈E​(H)Gσ​(i),σ​(j)subscript𝑋𝐻𝐺1aut𝐻subscript:𝜎→delimited-[]𝑣delimited-[]𝑛1-to-1subscriptproduct𝑖𝑗𝐸𝐻subscript𝐺𝜎𝑖𝜎𝑗X_{H}(G)=\frac{1}{|\operatorname{aut}(H)|}\sum_{\sigma:[v]\rightarrow[n]\text{1-to-1}}\prod_{(i,j)\in E(H)}G_{\sigma(i),\sigma(j)}. 

If we are lucky and H𝐻H appears in both G0subscript𝐺0G_{0} and G1subscript𝐺1G_{1}, then we can conclude that it is most likely that the vertices of H𝐻H in G0subscript𝐺0G_{0} are mapped to the vertices of H𝐻H in G1subscript𝐺1G_{1}, since the probability that both copies appear by chance is μ2superscript𝜇2\mu^{2}.This is much smaller than μ​(12)O​(1)𝜇superscript12𝑂1\mu(\tfrac{1}{2})^{O(1)}, which is the probability that H𝐻H appears in G0subscript𝐺0G_{0} and those edges were not dropped in G1subscript𝐺1G_{1}.If we are further lucky (or chose our swan carefully) so that H𝐻H has no non-trivial automorphism, then it turns out that we can in such a case deduce the precise permutation of the vertices of H𝐻H in G0subscript𝐺0G_{0} to the vertices of H𝐻H in G1subscript𝐺1G_{1}.

The above does not seem helpful in designing an algorithm to recover the permutation, or even to distinguish between 𝒟nullsubscript𝒟null\mathcal{D}_{\text{\tiny null}} and 𝒟structsubscript𝒟struct\mathcal{D}_{\text{\tiny struct}} since by its nature as a “black swan”, most of the times H𝐻H will not appear as a subgraph of G0subscript𝐺0G_{0}, and hence we would not be able to use it.Our approach is to use a flock of such swans, which are a set ℋℋ\mathcal{H} of graphs H1,…,Htsubscript𝐻1…subscript𝐻𝑡H_{1},\ldots,H_{t} such that the probability of every individual graph Hisubscript𝐻𝑖H_{i} occurring as a subgraph is very small, but the probability of some graph Hisubscript𝐻𝑖H_{i} occurring is very high.We carefully designate properties of the family (which we call the test graph family) so that when i≠j𝑖𝑗i\neq j the events that Hisubscript𝐻𝑖H_{i} and Hjsubscript𝐻𝑗H_{j} occur as subgraphs are roughly independent.

Already, this allows us to use the common occurrences of graphs in this family to deduce whether (G0,G1)subscript𝐺0subscript𝐺1(G_{0},G_{1}) came from the null distribution (in which case such occurrences will be rare) or whether they came from the structured distribution (in which case they will be more frequent).In particular, if we define ℋ={H1,…,Ht}ℋsubscript𝐻1…subscript𝐻𝑡\mathcal{H}=\{H_{1},\ldots,H_{t}\}, and the polynomialpℋ​(G0,G1)=∑H∈ℋ(XH​(G0)−μ)​(XH​(G1)−μ),subscript𝑝ℋsubscript𝐺0subscript𝐺1subscript𝐻ℋsubscript𝑋𝐻subscript𝐺0𝜇subscript𝑋𝐻subscript𝐺1𝜇p_{\mathcal{H}}(G_{0},G_{1})=\sum_{H\in\mathcal{H}}(X_{H}(G_{0})-\mu)(X_{H}(G_{1})-\mu),then the value of pℋsubscript𝑝ℋp_{\mathcal{H}} will be noticeably higher when (G0,G1)subscript𝐺0subscript𝐺1(G_{0},G_{1}) are drawn from 𝒟structsubscript𝒟struct\mathcal{D}_{\text{\tiny struct}} than when they are drawn from 𝒟nullsubscript𝒟null\mathcal{D}_{\text{\tiny null}}.This will result in an efficient algorithm to distinguish the two distributions.We also use the “swans” for recovery, as we will discuss below.

It turns out that demonstrating the existence999We note that since the graphs in the family will be of size v=O​(log⁡n)𝑣𝑂𝑛v=O(\log n), and counting the number of occurrences of a graph on v𝑣v vertices takes time nO​(v)superscript𝑛𝑂𝑣n^{O(v)}, once we fix v𝑣v we can perform brute-force enumeration over all graphs on v𝑣v vertices with negligible effect on the asymptotic runtime.For this reason, demonstrating the existence of a family is enough. (The construction need not be algorithmic, though ours is).of a family of “swans,” or “test graphs,” is a delicate task, as we need to satisfy several properties that are in opposition to one another.On one hand, we want the family to be large, so that we can compensate for the fact that each member is a “black swan” and appears with very small probability.On the other hand, we need each member of the family to have a small number of edges.Suppose that one of our swans, H𝐻H, has e𝑒e edges.If it appears in the base graph B𝐵B, then it only survives in both G0subscript𝐺0G_{0} and G1subscript𝐺1G_{1} with probability γ2​esuperscript𝛾2𝑒\gamma^{2e}.That is, the correlation between XH​(G0)subscript𝑋𝐻subscript𝐺0X_{H}(G_{0}) and XH​(G1)subscript𝑋𝐻subscript𝐺1X_{H}(G_{1}) decays exponentially in the number of edges of H𝐻H, and if H𝐻H is too large we cannot expect it to help us recover.As a side effect, keeping H𝐻H small helps with the computational efficiency of the task of finding these occurrences.A third constraint is that we need the events that each member of the family occurs to be roughly independent.It is very easy to come up with a large family of graphs for which the events of them co-occurring together are highly correlated, but such a family would not be useful for our algorithm.Ensuring this independence amounts to obtaining control over the edge density of the common subgraphs that appear in pairs of distinct test graphs.Luckily, we are able to demonstrate the existence of families of graphs achieving the desired properties, though this require some care.

The above discussion applies to the distinguishing problem of telling 𝒟nullsubscript𝒟null\mathcal{D}_{\text{\tiny null}} and 𝒟structsubscript𝒟struct\mathcal{D}_{\text{\tiny struct}} apart.However, if we can ensure that the joint occurrences of our family of test graphs cover all the vertices of G0subscript𝐺0G_{0} and G1subscript𝐺1G_{1}, then we can actually recover the permutation.This underlies our recovery algorithm.To simultaneously ensure these conditions we need to make the number of vertices of each Hisubscript𝐻𝑖H_{i} logarithmic rather than constant, which results in a quasipolynomial time algorithm.

We now describe more precisely (though still not in full formality, see Section 4) the properties that our family ℋℋ\mathcal{H} of “black swans” or test graphs needs to satisfy so the above algorithm will succeed:

Low likelihood of appearing.Each graph in our test family will have v𝑣v vertices and e𝑒e edges. To ensure that it is indeed a “black swan”, we require that nv​pe=μsuperscript𝑛𝑣superscript𝑝𝑒𝜇n^{v}p^{e}=\mu for μ𝜇\mu slightly subconstant. In particular, in the regime p=n−1+δ𝑝superscript𝑛1𝛿p=n^{-1+\delta} this will require e≥(1−δ)−1​v𝑒superscript1𝛿1𝑣e\geq(1-\delta)^{-1}v. In fact, we will set e𝑒e to be almost exactly (1−δ)−1​vsuperscript1𝛿1𝑣(1-\delta)^{-1}v. Note that when, say, δ<1/10𝛿110\delta<1/10 these are graphs with less than, say 109⋅v⋅109𝑣\tfrac{10}{9}\cdot v edges, so that the average degree is close to 222.Strict balance.This condition is well-known in the random graphs literature, and it ensures that the random variable XH​(G)subscript𝑋𝐻𝐺X_{H}(G) is well behaved. It states that for every H𝐻H in the family ℋℋ\mathcal{H}, every induced subgraph H′superscript𝐻′H^{\prime} of H𝐻H with v′superscript𝑣′v^{\prime} vertices and e′superscript𝑒′e^{\prime} edges has strictly smaller edge density, e′/v′<e/vsuperscript𝑒′superscript𝑣′𝑒𝑣e^{\prime}/v^{\prime}<e/v.We will actually require a strengthened, quantitative notion of strict balance, in which the density of H′superscript𝐻′H^{\prime} is related to its size.Intersection balance.To ensure that for every pair of distinct graphs H1,H2subscript𝐻1subscript𝐻2H_{1},H_{2} in our family the random variables XH1​(G)subscript𝑋subscript𝐻1𝐺X_{H_{1}}(G) and XH2​(G)subscript𝑋subscript𝐻2𝐺X_{H_{2}}(G) will be asymptotically independent when μ≪1much-less-than𝜇1\mu\ll 1, we will need to have even tighter control over the density of their common subgraphs. We will require that for every two such graphs, any subgraph H′superscript𝐻′H^{\prime} of their intersection satisfies the stronger condition that e′/v′<e/v−αsuperscript𝑒′superscript𝑣′𝑒𝑣𝛼e^{\prime}/v^{\prime}<e/v-\alpha for some sufficiently large α>0𝛼0\alpha>0.No non-trivial automorphism.To ensure we can recover the permutation correctly from an occurrence of H𝐻H in G0subscript𝐺0G_{0} and an occurrence in G1subscript𝐺1G_{1}, we require that every H𝐻H in ℋℋ\mathcal{H} has no non-trivial automorphism.Largeness.Finally to ensure that there actually will be many subgraphs from this family in our graph, we will require that |ℋ|​μ​γe>nℋ𝜇superscript𝛾𝑒𝑛|\mathcal{H}|\mu\gamma^{e}>n. (For distinguishing, it will suffice that |ℋ|​γ2​e=Ω​(1)ℋsuperscript𝛾2𝑒Ω1|\mathcal{H}|\gamma^{2e}=\Omega(1).)

We conjecture that a family achieving these properties can be obtained with any density e/v>1𝑒𝑣1e/v>1 (see Conjecture 1.3).However, at the moment we only demonstrate the existence of such families of graphs with certain densities, which is why our algorithms do not work for all ranges of p𝑝p.101010More accurately, we do have conjectured constructions that demonstrate the existence of such graphs for all densities, but have not yet been able to analyze them in all regimes.

We now illustrate one such construction.First and foremost, it can be shown that for integer d≥3𝑑3d\geq 3, random d𝑑d-regular graphs H𝐻H satisfy the requirements of strict balance and trivial automorphism group. Further, a sufficiently large fraction of the set of d𝑑d-regular random graphs will satisfy the intersection balance property.So for graphs with e=(1−δ)−1​v𝑒superscript1𝛿1𝑣e=(1-\delta)^{-1}v where (1−δ)−1=d/2superscript1𝛿1𝑑2(1-\delta)^{-1}=d/2, we easily have such a family.

However, the above construction does not give us graphs of all densities, and in particular does not allow us to handle the most interesting regime of sparse Erdös-Rényi correlated graphs (e.g., p<n−0.999𝑝superscript𝑛0.999p<n^{-0.999} or so) which requires test graph of density roughly 1+δ1𝛿1+\delta for some small δ𝛿\delta and in particular a non integer average degree of roughly 2+2​δ22𝛿2+2\delta.Here is one example for such a construction when δ𝛿\delta is 1/(3​k+3)13𝑘31/(3k+3) for some large integer k𝑘k.We start with a random 333-regular graph H′superscript𝐻′H^{\prime} on v′superscript𝑣′v^{\prime} vertices (and hence 1.5​v′1.5superscript𝑣′1.5v^{\prime} edges).We then subdivide every edge by inserting k𝑘k intermediate vertices into it, and so turning it into a path of length k+1𝑘1k+1.The resulting graph H𝐻H will have e=1.5​v′​(k+1)𝑒1.5superscript𝑣′𝑘1e=1.5v^{\prime}(k+1) edges and v=v′+k​e′=v′+1.5​k​v′𝑣superscript𝑣′𝑘superscript𝑒′superscript𝑣′1.5𝑘superscript𝑣′v=v^{\prime}+ke^{\prime}=v^{\prime}+1.5kv^{\prime} vertices, and one can verify that e=(1−δ)−1​v𝑒superscript1𝛿1𝑣e=(1-\delta)^{-1}v.Moreover, it can be shown that the densest subgraphs of H𝐻H will “respect” the underlying structure, in the sense that for every original edge of H′superscript𝐻′H^{\prime}, a subgraph of H𝐻H maximizing the density will either include all the corresponding path or none of it.Using this observation, and the expansion properties of random graphs, it is possible to show that strict balance condition and even the intersection balance condition hold.Moreover, we can also use known properties of random graphs to rule out non-trivial automorphism.Finally, since the number of 3-regular graphs on v′superscript𝑣′v^{\prime} vertices is v′⁣Ω​(v′)superscript𝑣′Ωsuperscript𝑣′v^{\prime\Omega(v^{\prime})}, for v′=Ω​(v)superscript𝑣′Ω𝑣v^{\prime}=\Omega(v) we get a super exponential (i.e., 2ω​(v)superscript2𝜔𝑣2^{\omega(v)}) number of graphs, which will allow us to get a sufficiently large family.We will furthermore need to make the notion of strict balance quantitative. For this and the remaining details, see Section 4, where we also give our constructions for other values of (1−δ)−1superscript1𝛿1(1-\delta)^{-1}.

As mentioned above, there is an extremely large body of literature on the graph matching problem. We discussed above the works on correlated Erdös-Rényi graphs, but people have also studied other generative models such as power law graphs and others (e.g., see [JLG+15]).

On a technical level, our work is inspired by recent works on sum-of-squares, and using low degree polynomials for inference problems [HKP+17].In particular, our starting point is a low degree distinguisher from the planted and structured distributions.However, there are some differences with the prior works.These works typically searched for objects such as cuts, vectors, or assignments that are less structured than searching for permutations.Moreover, unlike prior works where the polynomial distinguishers used fairly simple polynomials (such as counting edges, triangles, cycles, etc..), we need to use subgraphs with more complex structure.This is related to the fact that despite this inspiration, our algorithm at the moment is not a sum-of-squares algorithm.It remains an open problem whether the natural sum-of-squares relaxation for (1) captures our algorithm.

In our analysis we draw on the vast literature on analyzing the distribution of subgraph counts (e.g., see [JLR11]).Our setting is however somewhat different as we need to construct a family of graphs with related but not identical properties to those studied in prior works; in particular, some differences arise because of the fact that the graphs G0,G1subscript𝐺0subscript𝐺1G_{0},G_{1} are correlated, and the fact that we work in the regime where the graphs have size growing with n𝑛n and appear o​(1)𝑜1o(1) times in expectation.

In Section 2 we give our distinguishing algorithm between 𝒟nullsubscript𝒟null\mathcal{D}_{\text{\tiny null}} and 𝒟structsubscript𝒟struct\mathcal{D}_{\text{\tiny struct}} (Theorem 1.4).Then in Section 3 we build on this algorithm to obtain a recovery algorithm that recovers the “ground truth” permutation from (G0,G1)subscript𝐺0subscript𝐺1(G_{0},G_{1}) drawn from 𝒟structsubscript𝒟struct\mathcal{D}_{\text{\tiny struct}}.This algorithm builds upon and extends the techniques of our distinguishing algorithm.Both the recovery algorithm and distinguishing algorithms use as a “black box” the existence of families ℋℋ\mathcal{H} of “test graphs” (the black swans) that satisfy certain properties.In Section 4 we show how to construct such test families.

For a graph G=(V,E)𝐺𝑉𝐸G=(V,E) and a subset of the vertices S⊆V𝑆𝑉S\subseteq V, we use G​[S]𝐺delimited-[]𝑆G[S] to denote the vertex-induced subgraph on S𝑆S and we use E​[S]𝐸delimited-[]𝑆E[S] to denote the set of edges with both endpoints in S𝑆S. We use 𝕍​(X)𝕍𝑋\mathbb{V}(X) to denote the variance of the random variable X𝑋X. For an event ℰℰ\mathcal{E}, 𝕀⁡[ℰ]𝕀ℰ\operatorname{\mathbb{I}}[\mathcal{E}] is the 0-1 indicator that ℰℰ\mathcal{E} occurs. For a two graphs G,H𝐺𝐻G,H, we use G≅H𝐺𝐻G\cong H to indicate that G𝐺G and H𝐻H are isomorphic, and H⊆G𝐻𝐺H\subseteq G to indicate that G𝐺G contains H𝐻H as an edge-induced subgraph. We use nk¯superscript𝑛¯𝑘n^{\underline{k}} to denote the falling factorial, nk¯=n​(n−1)​⋯​(n−k+1)superscript𝑛¯𝑘𝑛𝑛1⋯𝑛𝑘1n^{\underline{k}}=n(n-1)\cdots(n-k+1).We will also use standard big-O𝑂O notation, and we will use f​(n)≪g​(n)much-less-than𝑓𝑛𝑔𝑛f(n)\ll g(n) to denote that limn→∞f​(n)g​(n)→0→subscript→𝑛𝑓𝑛𝑔𝑛0\lim_{n\to\infty}\frac{f(n)}{g(n)}\to 0.

In this section, we give an algorithm for the following distinguishing problem:

This section will be devoted to a proof of the following theorem, which is a generalization and directly implies Theorem 1.4:

Recall that for graphs G,H𝐺𝐻G,H, we define the subgraph count XH​(G)subscript𝑋𝐻𝐺X_{H}(G) to be the number of subgraphs of G𝐺G isomorphic to H𝐻H.Since G0,G1subscript𝐺0subscript𝐺1G_{0},G_{1} sampled from 𝒟structsubscript𝒟struct\mathcal{D}_{\text{\tiny struct}} are correlated, if a subgraph H𝐻H appears in G0subscript𝐺0G_{0} then it is more likely to also appear in G1subscript𝐺1G_{1}, and the subgraph counts are correlated.The following lemma uses this approach to give a certain “test”: a polynomial pH​(G0,G1)subscript𝑝𝐻subscript𝐺0subscript𝐺1p_{H}(G_{0},G_{1}) that has zero mean when (G0,G1)subscript𝐺0subscript𝐺1(G_{0},G_{1}) is chosen from the null distribution, but positive mean when they are chosen from the structured distribution.This test will not be good enough, since even in the structured case, it will be unlikely that the polynomial takes a non-zero value, but will serve as our starting point.

Lemma 2.3 guarantees that the count-deviation polynomial pHsubscript𝑝𝐻p_{H} has larger expected value under 𝒟structsubscript𝒟struct\mathcal{D}_{\text{\tiny struct}} than 𝒟nullsubscript𝒟null\mathcal{D}_{\text{\tiny null}}.However, this fact alone does not prove that pHsubscript𝑝𝐻p_{H} is a distinguisher.To illustrate this point, let us for simplicity suppose that we are in the regime where nv​(γ​p)e=Csuperscript𝑛𝑣superscript𝛾𝑝𝑒𝐶n^{v}({\gamma}p)^{e}=C for C𝐶C constant.In this case, Lemma 2.3 gives us that𝔼𝒟struct[pH​(G0,G1)]≈C⋅γe,subscript𝔼subscript𝒟structsubscript𝑝𝐻subscript𝐺0subscript𝐺1⋅𝐶superscript𝛾𝑒\operatorname*{\mathbb{E}}_{\mathcal{D}_{\text{\tiny struct}}}\big{[}p_{H}(G_{0},G_{1})\big{]}\approx C\cdot{\gamma}^{e},up to lower-order terms (assuming that H𝐻H has no subgraph K𝐾K with 𝔼XK​(G)<𝔼XH​(G)𝔼subscript𝑋𝐾𝐺𝔼subscript𝑋𝐻𝐺\operatorname*{\mathbb{E}}X_{K}(G)<\operatorname*{\mathbb{E}}X_{H}(G)).On the other hand, a simple calculation gives an optimistic bound on the standard deviation of pHsubscript𝑝𝐻p_{H} under 𝒟nullsubscript𝒟null\mathcal{D}_{\text{\tiny null}} of𝕍𝒟null​(pH​(G0,G1))1/2≈C.subscript𝕍subscript𝒟nullsuperscriptsubscript𝑝𝐻subscript𝐺0subscript𝐺112𝐶\mathbb{V}_{\mathcal{D}_{\text{\tiny null}}}\left(p_{H}(G_{0},G_{1})\right)^{1/2}\approx C.So the standard deviation in the null case is too large for us to reliably detect the offset expectation.

Our solution is to identify a “test set” of graphs ℋℋ\mathcal{H}, such that the estimators pHsubscript𝑝𝐻p_{H} for H∈ℋ𝐻ℋH\in\mathcal{H} are close to independent.121212Here we mean in the sense that the variance of their average is asymptotically equal to an average of independent estimators.If we had |ℋ|=Tℋ𝑇|\mathcal{H}|=T trials, intuitively we expect the standard deviation to decrease by a factor of T𝑇\sqrt{T}.So long as we satisfyCT<C⋅γe,𝐶𝑇⋅𝐶superscript𝛾𝑒\frac{C}{\sqrt{T}}<C\cdot{\gamma}^{e},the variance in the null case may be sufficiently small that we reliably distinguish.

In order to translate this cartoon sketch into a reality, we will require some additional properties of our test set which will be crucial in controlling the variance.

Given a graph H𝐻H with e𝑒e edges and v𝑣v vertices, in expectation there are nv¯⋅pe/aut⁡(H)⋅superscript𝑛¯𝑣superscript𝑝𝑒aut𝐻n^{\underline{v}}\cdot p^{e}/\operatorname{aut}(H) copies of H𝐻H in 𝔾⁡(n,p)𝔾𝑛𝑝\operatorname{\mathbb{G}}(n,p).Because of our prevailing intuition that random graphs are well-behaved, we might naively expect that the number of copies of H𝐻H is concentrated around its mean; however some simple examples demonstrate that this is not always the case.

Consider for example the graph H𝐻H given by appending a “hair”, or a path of length 111, to a clique of size 444 (see Fig. 3).H𝐻H has 555 vertices, 777 edges, and 3!33! automorphisms, so in G​(n,p)𝐺𝑛𝑝G(n,p) with p=2​n−5/7𝑝2superscript𝑛57p=2n^{-5/7}, we have𝔼G∼𝔾⁡(n,p)[XH​(G)]=13!⋅n5¯⋅p7=273!⋅(1−on​(1)).subscript𝔼similar-to𝐺𝔾𝑛𝑝subscript𝑋𝐻𝐺⋅13superscript𝑛¯5superscript𝑝7⋅superscript2731subscript𝑜𝑛1\operatorname*{\mathbb{E}}_{G\sim\operatorname{\mathbb{G}}(n,p)}[X_{H}(G)]=\frac{1}{3!}\cdot n^{\underline{5}}\cdot p^{7}=\frac{2^{7}}{3!}\cdot(1-o_{n}(1)).So in expectation, H𝐻H appears in G𝐺G 273!superscript273\tfrac{2^{7}}{3!} times.

However, if we restrict our attention to the clique K4subscript𝐾4K_{4} which is a subgraph of H𝐻H, in expectation𝔼G∼𝔾⁡(n,p)[XK4​(G)]=14!⋅n4¯⋅p6=O​(n−2/7).subscript𝔼similar-to𝐺𝔾𝑛𝑝subscript𝑋subscript𝐾4𝐺⋅14superscript𝑛¯4superscript𝑝6𝑂superscript𝑛27\operatorname*{\mathbb{E}}_{G\sim\operatorname{\mathbb{G}}(n,p)}[X_{K_{4}}(G)]=\frac{1}{4!}\cdot n^{\underline{4}}\cdot p^{6}=O(n^{-2/7}).So while the expected number of copies of H𝐻H is constant, K4subscript𝐾4K_{4} is not expected to appear even once!The large expected count of H𝐻H is due to a small-probability event; if K4subscript𝐾4K_{4} appears (with polynomially small probability), then we will see many copies of H𝐻H.

This issue is well known in the study of random graphs (it is among the first topics in the textbook of Janson et al. [JLR11]).From that well-developed literature, we borrow the following concept:

If a graph H𝐻H is expected to appear at least once, then the balancedness of a graph H𝐻H is what determines whether the number of copies of H𝐻H in 𝔾⁡(n,p)𝔾𝑛𝑝\operatorname{\mathbb{G}}(n,p) is well-concentrated around its mean.For example, Lemma 2.3 already allows us the following observation:

To ensure asymptotic independence, we will require that each of the graphs n our test set ℋℋ\mathcal{H} be strictly balanced.

We will prove this proposition in Section 4; for now, assuming we have such a family, we proceed to prove Theorem 1.4.

We are now ready to prove that there is a poly-time computable distinguishing polynomial with bounded variance.The existing results on concentration of subgraph counts is not sufficient for us here, because we are interested in the setting of correlated graphs.We will bound the variance directly.

Choose v𝑣v to be a sufficiently large even integer such that vc>400/(γ)2superscript𝑣𝑐400superscript𝛾2v^{c}>400/(\gamma)^{2}, where c𝑐c is the constant from Theorem 4.1 so that |ℋ|≥vc1−δ​vℋsuperscript𝑣𝑐1𝛿𝑣|\mathcal{H}|\geq v^{\frac{c}{1-\delta}v}.Let ℋℋ\mathcal{H} be the test set of subgraphs guaranteed by Theorem 4.1 with v𝑣v vertices and e𝑒e edges, so that ev=(1−δ)−1𝑒𝑣superscript1𝛿1\frac{e}{v}=(1-\delta)^{-1}.By this setting of parameters we have then that 𝔼[XH​(G0)]=1±o​(1)𝔼subscript𝑋𝐻subscript𝐺0plus-or-minus1𝑜1\operatorname*{\mathbb{E}}[X_{H}(G_{0})]=1\pm o(1).

The bounds from Lemma 2.3 gives us the expectation of P𝑃P under 𝒟nullsubscript𝒟null\mathcal{D}_{\text{\tiny null}} and 𝒟structsubscript𝒟struct\mathcal{D}_{\text{\tiny struct}}.Using the balancedness properties of our test set, we will bound the variance of P𝑃P under 𝒟nullsubscript𝒟null\mathcal{D}_{\text{\tiny null}} and 𝒟structsubscript𝒟struct\mathcal{D}_{\text{\tiny struct}}.

H^{\prime}}])(\mathbf{1}_{C\supseteq H}-\operatorname*{\mathbb{E}}[\mathbf{1}_{C\supseteq H}])(\mathbf{1}_{D\supseteq H^{\prime}}-\operatorname*{\mathbb{E}}[\mathbf{1}_{D\supseteq H^{\prime}}])\big{]}.(18)Rather than expand the product above into 16 terms, we liken it to a process of inclusion-exclusion.We have already seen that sums of the form ∑𝔼[𝟏A⊇H​𝟏B⊇H′]𝔼subscript1𝐻𝐴subscript1superscript𝐻′𝐵\sum\operatorname*{\mathbb{E}}[\mathbf{1}_{A\supseteq H}\mathbf{1}_{B\supseteq H^{\prime}}] are equivalent to weighted sums over subgraphs J⊆H,H′𝐽𝐻superscript𝐻′J\subseteq H,H^{\prime}, which correspond to different ways of taking the union of the graphs H,H′𝐻superscript𝐻′H,H^{\prime} by choosing the intersection to be equal to the subgraph J𝐽J.Specifically, the weight for each subgraph J𝐽J is proportional to n−|V​(J)|​p−|E​(J)|superscript𝑛𝑉𝐽superscript𝑝𝐸𝐽n^{-|V(J)|}p^{-|E(J)|}.The term ∑𝔼[𝟏A⊇H]​𝔼[𝟏B⊇H′]𝔼subscript1𝐻𝐴𝔼subscript1superscript𝐻′𝐵\sum\operatorname*{\mathbb{E}}[\mathbf{1}_{A\supseteq H}]\operatorname*{\mathbb{E}}[\mathbf{1}_{B\supseteq H^{\prime}}], on the other hand, corresponds to a similar sum, but the weight for the J𝐽Jth term is proportional merely to n−|V​(J)|superscript𝑛𝑉𝐽n^{-|V(J)|}.For terms in which J𝐽J contains no edges, these weights are equivalent; when |E​(J)|>0𝐸𝐽0|E(J)|>0, the weight of the first term dominates the weight of the second.Therefore, when the second term is subtracted from the first, we are “exclude” the intersections J𝐽J which contain no edges (up to lower order terms).

An analogous phenomenon transpires above.The leading term, 𝔼[𝟏A⊇H​𝟏B⊇H′​𝟏C⊇H​𝟏B⊇H′]𝔼subscript1𝐻𝐴subscript1superscript𝐻′𝐵subscript1𝐻𝐶subscript1superscript𝐻′𝐵\operatorname*{\mathbb{E}}[\mathbf{1}_{A\supseteq H}\mathbf{1}_{B\supseteq H^{\prime}}\mathbf{1}_{C\supseteq H}\mathbf{1}_{B\supseteq H^{\prime}}] includes all ways of intersecting the two copies of H𝐻H and the two copies of H′superscript𝐻′H^{\prime}.The following “order-1 exclusion term”, 𝔼[𝟏A⊇H]​𝔼[𝟏B⊇H′​𝟏C⊇H​𝟏D⊇H′]𝔼subscript1𝐻𝐴𝔼subscript1superscript𝐻′𝐵subscript1𝐻𝐶subscript1superscript𝐻′𝐷\operatorname*{\mathbb{E}}[\mathbf{1}_{A\supseteq H}]\operatorname*{\mathbb{E}}[\mathbf{1}_{B\supseteq H^{\prime}}\mathbf{1}_{C\supseteq H}\mathbf{1}_{D\supseteq H^{\prime}}], is subtracted to exclude terms in which the copy of H𝐻H in G0subscript𝐺0G_{0} does not intersect with any of the other subgraphs on any edges.After subtracting all three of the order-1 exclusion terms, we must add the six “order-2 inclusion terms”, (e.g. 𝔼[𝟏A⊇H]​𝔼[𝟏B⊇H′]​𝔼[𝟏C⊇H​𝟏D⊇H′]𝔼subscript1𝐻𝐴𝔼subscript1superscript𝐻′𝐵𝔼subscript1𝐻𝐶subscript1superscript𝐻′𝐷\operatorname*{\mathbb{E}}[\mathbf{1}_{A\supseteq H}]\operatorname*{\mathbb{E}}[\mathbf{1}_{B\supseteq H^{\prime}}]\operatorname*{\mathbb{E}}[\mathbf{1}_{C\supseteq H}\mathbf{1}_{D\supseteq H^{\prime}}]), and so on, until we are finally left with a summation in which every copy of H𝐻H and H′superscript𝐻′H^{\prime} must intersect with at least one other subgraph on at least one edge.131313For example, if H𝐻H in G0subscript𝐺0G_{0} contains the edge (u,v)𝑢𝑣(u,v), and H′superscript𝐻′H^{\prime} in G1subscript𝐺1G_{1} contains the edge (π​(u),π​(v))𝜋𝑢𝜋𝑣(\pi(u),\pi(v)), this term is included in the summation. On the other hand if H𝐻H in G0subscript𝐺0G_{0} does not share any edges with the second copy of H𝐻H in G1subscript𝐺1G_{1} or either copy of H′superscript𝐻′H^{\prime}, the term is excluded.

Given Theorem 2.6, we can complete our algorithm and prove Theorem 2.2:

The algorithm is as follows: compute the value of P​(G0,G1)𝑃subscript𝐺0subscript𝐺1P(G_{0},G_{1}), and if the value is larger than 13​𝔼𝒟struct[P​(G0,G1)]13subscript𝔼subscript𝒟struct𝑃subscript𝐺0subscript𝐺1\frac{1}{3}\operatorname*{\mathbb{E}}_{\mathcal{D}_{\text{\tiny struct}}}[P(G_{0},G_{1})], say that G0subscript𝐺0G_{0} and G1subscript𝐺1G_{1} come from 𝒟structsubscript𝒟struct\mathcal{D}_{\text{\tiny struct}}, otherwise say that G0subscript𝐺0G_{0} and G1subscript𝐺1G_{1} come from 𝒟nullsubscript𝒟null\mathcal{D}_{\text{\tiny null}}.Clearly the algorithm runs in polynomial time: it requires counting the number of occurrences of constant-sized subgraphs in two graphs of size n𝑛n.

The claim is that in both cases, we are correct with probability at least 0.990.990.99.141414Notice that had we taken v=ω​(1)𝑣𝜔1v=\omega(1), we could amplify this probability to 1−o​(1)1𝑜11-o(1).The reason is that if we apply Chebyshev’s inequality to P​(G0,G1)𝑃subscript𝐺0subscript𝐺1P(G_{0},G_{1}), by Theorem 2.6 and Lemma 2.3 we have that in the structured distribution, P​(G0,G1)𝑃subscript𝐺0subscript𝐺1P(G_{0},G_{1}) is unlikely to be too small:Pr𝒟struct⁡(P​(G0,G1)<12​𝔼𝒟struct[P​(G0,G1)])≤41600.subscriptPrsubscript𝒟struct𝑃subscript𝐺0subscript𝐺112subscript𝔼subscript𝒟struct𝑃subscript𝐺0subscript𝐺141600\Pr_{\mathcal{D}_{\text{\tiny struct}}}\left(P(G_{0},G_{1})<\frac{1}{2}\operatorname*{\mathbb{E}}_{\mathcal{D}_{\text{\tiny struct}}}[P(G_{0},G_{1})]\right)\leq\frac{4}{1600}.At the same time, Chebyshev’s inequality also guarantees that P​(G0,G1)𝑃subscript𝐺0subscript𝐺1P(G_{0},G_{1}) is not too large with good probability:Pr𝒟null⁡(P​(G0,G1)≥14​𝔼𝒟struct[P​(G0,G1)])≤161600.subscriptPrsubscript𝒟null𝑃subscript𝐺0subscript𝐺114subscript𝔼subscript𝒟struct𝑃subscript𝐺0subscript𝐺1161600\Pr_{\mathcal{D}_{\text{\tiny null}}}\left(P(G_{0},G_{1})\geq\frac{1}{4}\operatorname*{\mathbb{E}}_{\mathcal{D}_{\text{\tiny struct}}}[P(G_{0},G_{1})]\right)\leq\frac{16}{1600}.This concludes the proof.∎

In this section, we will solve the following recovery problem:

We give a quasipolynomial time algorithm for the above problem; below we state the precise guarantees.

Note that Theorem 3.2 implies Theorem 1.1.

Our algorithm first finds a rough estimate of π∗superscript𝜋\pi^{*} using test subgraphs (as we did for our distinguishing algorithm), then completes the estimate via a boosting procedure.We fill in the details of our strategy in Sections 3.1 and 3.2.In Sections 3.3, 3.4, 3.5 and 3.6, we prove that our algorithm successfully estimates π∗superscript𝜋\pi^{*}, and in Section 3.7 we give the boosting algorithm.Finally, we tie everything together and prove Theorem 3.2 in Section 3.8.

For the purposes of distinguishing, we used the counts of subgraphs belonging to a specially constructed “test set” of strictly balanced graphs with no non-trivial automorphisms.However, we only used subgraphs of size O​(1)𝑂1O(1) to distinguish, each of which crucially appeared only O​(1)𝑂1{O}(1) times.For a fixed constant size s𝑠s, there are only 2s2superscript2superscript𝑠22^{s^{2}} possible subgraphs on s𝑠s vertices; if each appears only with multiplicity O​(1)𝑂1{O}(1), most vertices in the graph will not participate in such subgraphs.

To recover the permutation π∗superscript𝜋\pi^{*}, we will use the same “test set” of subgraphs we used for distinguishing, only now we will choose the subgraphs to have size Ω​(log⁡n)Ω𝑛\Omega(\log n) (and consequently we will have a larger test set, so that more vertices are covered).Also, we will set their density so that every subgraph which does appear in both G0subscript𝐺0G_{0} and G1subscript𝐺1G_{1} almost certainly appears exactly once.If we see an occurrence of such a subgraph H𝐻H in both G0subscript𝐺0G_{0} and G1subscript𝐺1G_{1}, we can be confident that the vertices of H𝐻H in G0subscript𝐺0G_{0} and G1subscript𝐺1G_{1} correspond to each other in π∗superscript𝜋\pi^{*}.Finally, we fix any mis-matched vertices with a boosting procedure.

We require stronger conditions from our test set, which we delineate below.

Because our constructions differ depending on the value of d′superscript𝑑′d^{\prime}, we have stated the theorem in generality here; for precise statements, see Section 4.

The recovery algorithm consists of four steps.

To prove that Algorithm 3.4 works, we’ll establish the following three claims:•The probability that a vertex u∈V𝑢𝑉u\in V appears in a test graph H∈ℋ𝐻ℋH\in\mathcal{H} which appears more than once in the base graph B𝐵B, and also appears at least once in both G0,G1subscript𝐺0subscript𝐺1G_{0},G_{1}, is small.•Each vertex u∈V𝑢𝑉u\in V appears in some test graph H∈ℋ𝐻ℋH\in\mathcal{H} in both G0,G1subscript𝐺0subscript𝐺1G_{0},G_{1} with large enough probability.Since most H∈ℋ𝐻ℋH\in\mathcal{H} that appear in both G0,G1subscript𝐺0subscript𝐺1G_{0},G_{1} will appear uniquely in the base graph B𝐵B, together, these claims imply that in step 2 we will match many vertices to each other, and make few mistakes.•Given a one-to-one map between an Ω~​(1)~Ω1\tilde{\Omega}(1)-fraction of the vertices of G0,G1subscript𝐺0subscript𝐺1G_{0},G_{1} which is consistent with π∗superscript𝜋\pi^{*} on all but o​(1)𝑜1o(1) of the matches, the boosting algorithm recovers the optimal permutation π∗superscript𝜋\pi^{*}.

In this subsection, we will prove that few test graphs H∈ℋ𝐻ℋH\in\mathcal{H} appear more than once in the base graph, and also survive the subsampling in both G0subscript𝐺0G_{0} and G1subscript𝐺1G_{1}.We will bound the probability of this event in a straightforward manner, taking advantage of properties of the test graphs in ℋℋ\mathcal{H}.

By linearity of expectation, summing over all H∈ℋ𝐻ℋH\in\mathcal{H}, the number of H𝐻H which u𝑢u participates in that have at least two distinct appearances in B𝐵B and survive at least once in each of G0,G1subscript𝐺0subscript𝐺1G_{0},G_{1} is at most (by Eq. 23):𝔼[Bu]=𝔼[∑H∈ℋBuH]≤∑H∈ℋPr⁡[BuH]≤|ℋ|⋅v⋅nv−1​qe⋅O​(v−1/2)𝔼subscript𝐵𝑢𝔼subscript𝐻ℋsubscriptsuperscript𝐵𝐻𝑢subscript𝐻ℋPrsuperscriptsubscript𝐵𝑢𝐻⋅⋅ℋ𝑣superscript𝑛𝑣1superscript𝑞𝑒𝑂superscript𝑣12\operatorname*{\mathbb{E}}[B_{u}]=\operatorname*{\mathbb{E}}\left[\sum_{H\in\mathcal{H}}B^{H}_{u}\right]\leq\sum_{H\in\mathcal{H}}\Pr\left[B_{u}^{H}\right]\leq|\mathcal{H}|\cdot v\cdot n^{v-1}q^{e}\cdot O\left(v^{-1/2}\right)which completes the proof for the sparse case.

In the dense case, since J𝐽J is always a strict subgraph of H𝐻H, we can now apply Lemma 3.7 (since we have assumed that 2​v2+2​(d′+2)​q150≤v2​(d′+2)​n​qd′/2≤12​q−1502superscript𝑣22superscript𝑑′2superscript𝑞150superscript𝑣2superscript𝑑′2𝑛superscript𝑞superscript𝑑′212superscript𝑞1502v^{2+2(d^{\prime}+2)}q^{\frac{1}{50}}\leq v^{2(d^{\prime}+2)}nq^{d^{\prime}/2}\leq\frac{1}{2}q^{-\frac{1}{50}})to bound Eq. 22:Eq. 22≤4​v⋅n2​v−1​γ2​e​q2​e⋅4⋅(v2​(d′+2)​p150)⋅(1n​pd′/2)v−1=4​v⋅nv−1​qe⋅4​(v2​(d′+2)⋅n​p12​d′+150).absent⋅⋅4𝑣superscript𝑛2𝑣1superscript𝛾2𝑒superscript𝑞2𝑒4superscript𝑣2superscript𝑑′2superscript𝑝150superscript1𝑛superscript𝑝superscript𝑑′2𝑣1⋅⋅4𝑣superscript𝑛𝑣1superscript𝑞𝑒4⋅superscript𝑣2superscript𝑑′2𝑛superscript𝑝12superscript𝑑′150\displaystyle\leq 4v\cdot n^{2v-1}\gamma^{2e}q^{2e}\cdot 4\cdot(v^{2(d^{\prime}+2)}p^{\frac{1}{50}})\cdot\left(\frac{1}{np^{d^{\prime}/2}}\right)^{v-1}=4v\cdot n^{v-1}q^{e}\cdot 4\left(v^{2(d^{\prime}+2)}\cdot np^{\frac{1}{2}d^{\prime}+\frac{1}{50}}\right).(24)where we have used that q=p​γ2𝑞𝑝superscript𝛾2q=p\gamma^{2} and e=d′2​v𝑒superscript𝑑′2𝑣e=\frac{d^{\prime}}{2}v.

Therefore we have bounded the probability that each vertex participates in a test graph H𝐻H introduces errors in the map constructed by the algorithm (that is, which appears more than once in B𝐵B and survives subsampling in both graphs).

To guarantee that the algorithm recovers a Ω~​(1)~Ω1\tilde{\Omega}(1) fraction of π∗superscript𝜋\pi^{*}, we will show that many vertices in B𝐵B are incident on a copy of H𝐻H which is present in both G0subscript𝐺0G_{0} and G1subscript𝐺1G_{1}.It will be convenient for us to think of the intersection graph of G0subscript𝐺0G_{0} and G1subscript𝐺1G_{1}:

We will lower bound the probability that a vertex is contained in at least one test graph inside the intersection graph.

To bound Eq. 28, we need to use the property of our graph family that for every H≠H′𝐻superscript𝐻′H\neq H^{\prime} inside the family, the density of the densest common subgraph is appreciably less than the density of H𝐻H.

Again because our guarantees differ depending on the density of H𝐻H, we bound this quantity separately for the case when H𝐻H has density at most 1.51.51.5 and the case when H𝐻H has density at least 333.

We will prove these lemmas in Section 3.6; with these bounds in hand, we complete the proof of the theorem.

From Lemma 3.9, we know that with good probability each vertex u𝑢u will be covered by at least one graph from the test set ℋℋ\mathcal{H} in the intersection graph.

For each u∈V𝑢𝑉u\in V, let Nusubscript𝑁𝑢N_{u} be the number of distinct H∈ℋ𝐻ℋH\in\mathcal{H} which contain the vertex u𝑢u and which appear in both G0,G1subscript𝐺0subscript𝐺1G_{0},G_{1}.In both the sparse and dense regimes, we have that 𝔼[Nu]=|ℋ|⋅v​nv−1​qe𝔼subscript𝑁𝑢⋅ℋ𝑣superscript𝑛𝑣1superscript𝑞𝑒\operatorname*{\mathbb{E}}[N_{u}]=|\mathcal{H}|\cdot vn^{v-1}q^{e}, which we have set to be at least vΩ​(v)superscript𝑣Ω𝑣v^{\Omega(v)} (where the ΩΩ\Omega hides a dependence on λ𝜆\lambda in the sparse case).

By Lemma 3.9, 𝕍​(Nu)=o​(𝔼[Nu])𝕍subscript𝑁𝑢𝑜𝔼subscript𝑁𝑢\mathbb{V}(N_{u})=o(\operatorname*{\mathbb{E}}[N_{u}]).The claim follows by Chebyshev’s inequality.∎

Let 𝕀usubscript𝕀𝑢\mathbb{I}_{u} be the indicator that u𝑢u is contained in at least 12​𝔼[Nu]12𝔼subscript𝑁𝑢\frac{1}{2}\operatorname*{\mathbb{E}}[N_{u}] such H𝐻H.With Claim 3.16 we have that ∑u∈V𝔼[𝕀u]≥(1−o​(1))​nsubscript𝑢𝑉𝔼subscript𝕀𝑢1𝑜1𝑛\sum_{u\in V}\operatorname*{\mathbb{E}}[\mathbb{I}_{u}]\geq(1-o(1))n.We can then apply the following averaging argument to conclude that with probability 1−θ−o​(1)1𝜃𝑜11-\theta-o(1), a θ𝜃\theta-fraction of the vertices are covered by at least one H∈ℋ𝐻ℋH\in\mathcal{H}:(1−o​(1))​n1𝑜1𝑛\displaystyle(1-o(1))n≤∑u∈V𝔼G0,G1[𝕀u]=𝔼[∑u∈V𝕀u]≤θ⋅n​Pr⁡[∑u∈V𝕀u<θ​n]+n⋅Pr⁡[∑u∈V𝕀u≥θ​n].absentsubscript𝑢𝑉subscript𝔼subscript𝐺0subscript𝐺1subscript𝕀𝑢𝔼subscript𝑢𝑉subscript𝕀𝑢⋅𝜃𝑛Prsubscript𝑢𝑉subscript𝕀𝑢𝜃𝑛⋅𝑛Prsubscript𝑢𝑉subscript𝕀𝑢𝜃𝑛\displaystyle\leq\sum_{u\in V}\operatorname*{\mathbb{E}}_{G_{0},G_{1}}[\mathbb{I}_{u}]=\operatorname*{\mathbb{E}}\left[\sum_{u\in V}\mathbb{I}_{u}\right]\leq\theta\cdot n\Pr\left[\sum_{u\in V}\mathbb{I}_{u}<\theta n\right]+n\cdot\Pr\left[\sum_{u\in V}\mathbb{I}_{u}\geq\theta n\right].Thus, Pr⁡[∑u∈V𝕀u≥θ​n]≥1−θ−o​(1)Prsubscript𝑢𝑉subscript𝕀𝑢𝜃𝑛1𝜃𝑜1\Pr\left[\sum_{u\in V}\mathbb{I}_{u}\geq\theta n\right]\geq 1-\theta-o(1) as desired.Taking θ=1log⁡v𝜃1𝑣\theta=\frac{1}{\log v} gives us the desired conclusion.∎

Let 𝕁usubscript𝕁𝑢\mathbb{J}_{u} be the event that Bu≥v−1/4​𝔼[Nu]subscript𝐵𝑢superscript𝑣14𝔼subscript𝑁𝑢B_{u}\geq v^{-1/4}\operatorname*{\mathbb{E}}[N_{u}].From Lemma 3.5 and Markov’s inequality, we have thatPr⁡[𝕁u]≤𝔼[Bu]v−1/4​𝔼[Nu]≤v−1/2​𝔼[Nu]v−1/4​𝔼[Nu]≤O​(v−1/4).Prsubscript𝕁𝑢𝔼subscript𝐵𝑢superscript𝑣14𝔼subscript𝑁𝑢superscript𝑣12𝔼subscript𝑁𝑢superscript𝑣14𝔼subscript𝑁𝑢𝑂superscript𝑣14\Pr[\mathbb{J}_{u}]\leq\frac{\operatorname*{\mathbb{E}}[B_{u}]}{v^{-1/4}\operatorname*{\mathbb{E}}[N_{u}]}\leq\frac{v^{-1/2}\operatorname*{\mathbb{E}}[N_{u}]}{v^{-1/4}\operatorname*{\mathbb{E}}[N_{u}]}\leq O(v^{-1/4}).

Then by Markov’s inequality we getPr⁡[∑u∈V𝕀u≥nlog2⁡v]≤O​(v−1/4​log2⁡v),Prsubscript𝑢𝑉subscript𝕀𝑢𝑛superscript2𝑣𝑂superscript𝑣14superscript2𝑣\Pr\left[\sum_{u\in V}\mathbb{I}_{u}\geq\frac{n}{\log^{2}v}\right]\leq O(v^{-1/4}\log^{2}v),as desired.∎

In our algorithm, independently for every vertex which participates in at least 12​𝔼[Nu]12𝔼subscript𝑁𝑢\frac{1}{2}\operatorname*{\mathbb{E}}[N_{u}] distinct H∈ℋ𝐻ℋH\in\mathcal{H} that appear in both G0,G1subscript𝐺0subscript𝐺1G_{0},G_{1}, we choose a uniformly random H∈ℋ𝐻ℋH\in\mathcal{H} that it participates in, and match it based on that copy of H𝐻H.

Given these conditions, Claim 3.17 (using Lemma 3.9) proves that step 2 of Algorithm 3.4 will match at least an Ω​(1log⁡v)Ω1𝑣\Omega(\frac{1}{\log v}) fraction of the vertices of G0,G1subscript𝐺0subscript𝐺1G_{0},G_{1}; since the H∈ℋ𝐻ℋH\in\mathcal{H} have no non-trivial automorphisms, the matches are always correct (and one-to-one) unless H𝐻H is “bad”, i.e. there is more than one copy of the H𝐻H that was chosen in the base graph B𝐵B.

By Claim 3.18, with high probability the proportion of such “bad” H𝐻H incident on each u∈V𝑢𝑉u\in V in the intersection graph is at most log2⁡vv1/4superscript2𝑣superscript𝑣14\frac{\log^{2}v}{v^{1/4}}; since we make the choice of mapping for each vertex independently, a Chernoff bound implies that for all but a v−1/8superscript𝑣18{v^{-1/8}} fraction of the matched vertices, we will make the match based on an H𝐻H that appears only once in the base graph B𝐵B.This completes the proof.∎

Let us start with an observation on the number of non-trivial automorphsims for a vertex-induced subgraph of a graph that has no non-trivial automorphism.

Let S⊂V​(J)𝑆𝑉𝐽S\subset V(J) be the ordered set of endpoints of the ℓℓ\ell edges removed.We have |S|≤2​ℓ𝑆2ℓ|S|\leq 2\ell.Now suppose by way of contradiction that J𝐽J has more than v2​ℓsuperscript𝑣2ℓv^{2\ell} non-trivial automorphisms.Then by a pigeonhole argument, there must be an ordered set of vertices T⊂V​(J)𝑇𝑉𝐽T\subset V(J) and two distinct automorphisms, π𝜋\pi and σ𝜎\sigma, such that π​(S)=σ​(S)=T𝜋𝑆𝜎𝑆𝑇\pi(S)=\sigma(S)=T.But then, π∘σ−1𝜋superscript𝜎1\pi\circ\sigma^{-1} is a non-trivial automorphism of J𝐽J that maps S𝑆S to S𝑆S, and can therefore be extended to a nontrivial automorphism of H𝐻H.This is a contradiction.∎

So far, we have shown that our algorithm partially recovers the ground truth permutation (with some errors).In this section we show how to “boost” this to full recovery of the ground truth permutation.

There are many algorithms in the literature on the graph matching problem that deal with recovering the ground truth permutation from such a partial matching on a subset of the vertices, which is typically known as a seed set [YG13, LFP14, LAV+14].These works typically study a simple percolation, which can be shown to succeed in a random graph [JŁT+12].However, our setting is somewhat different:

•These algorithms typically assume that the seed set is chosen at random, while in our setting the seed set (i.e., partial solution) is learned from the graph and has arbitrary correlation with it.•These algorithms typically (though not always [KHG15]) assume that the permutation is known perfectly on the seed set (i.e., that they are given an (s,1)𝑠1(s,1) partial solution), while it will be more convenient for us to allow some probability of error.

For this reason, we reprove here that a percolation-like algorithm succeeds in our more general setting (albeit with worse bounds than those of [JŁT+12]).Specifically, we show that we can boost an (Ω~​(n),1−o​(1))~Ω𝑛1𝑜1(\tilde{\Omega}(n),1-o(1)) partial solution to a the full ground truth:

Lemma 3.21 completes the proof of Theorem 3.2. Note the order of quantifiers in Lemma 3.21: the partial solution π𝜋\pi can be adversarially chosen, as long as it satisfies the desired conditions.The proof of Lemma 3.21 is obtained by analyzing the following percolation algorithm.This is the same algorithm used in prior works except that we set our parameters in a particular way, and the “seed set” can depend arbitrarily on the graph.We will denote by G𝐺G the “intersection graph” of G0subscript𝐺0G_{0} and G1subscript𝐺1G_{1} (see Definition 3.8). Note that G𝐺G is distributed according to 𝔾⁡(n,q)𝔾𝑛𝑞\operatorname{\mathbb{G}}(n,q) where q=γ2​p𝑞superscript𝛾2𝑝q=\gamma^{2}p.We let c𝑐c be some sufficiently large constant.

This algorithm will indeed run in polynomial time: In Step 1 we keep increasing the number of inputs on which π𝜋\pi is defined, and in Step 2 we increase in every iteration the potential ∑uN​(u,π​(u))subscript𝑢𝑁𝑢𝜋𝑢\sum_{u}N(u,\pi(u)) and so we can only run in polynomial time.Lemma 3.21 will follow from the following two claims:

If u≠v𝑢𝑣u\neq v, then their neighbors in G𝐺G are chosen independently at random and the expected number of them is (γ​p)2​nsuperscript𝛾𝑝2𝑛(\gamma p)^{2}n. In particular this means that at the end of step 1, at most 20​ϵ​θ​n20italic-ϵ𝜃𝑛20\epsilon\theta n vertices are undefined and for them we complete the permutation arbitrarily.∎

In this step we assume we have an almost optimal permutation π𝜋\pi such that π​(v)=π∗​(v)𝜋𝑣superscript𝜋𝑣\pi(v)=\pi^{*}(v) for all but δ𝛿\delta fraction of v𝑣v’s (for δ=O​(ϵ​θ)𝛿𝑂italic-ϵ𝜃\delta=O(\epsilon\theta))161616In the previous version, we did not take care of the possibility of some weird correlation between the error pattern and the almost optimal permutation we are starting with..

The main idea is that with high probability over the choice of random graphs, they will satisfy expansion properties that guarantee that our boosting algorithm does not get stuck.Note that this is with respect to an arbitrary initial error pattern. That is, with high probability over the choice of random graphs, no matter which almost optimal permutation we are starting with, the algorithm will not get stuck.Concretely, the following lemma shows that with high probability over the choice of random graphs, Step 2: Fixing the solution can keep making progress in fixing mismatched vertices:

Before we prove 3.27, note that it addresses the reviewer’s (valid!) concern in our original proof of Claim 3.22. The reason is that Step 2: Fixing the solution is able to keep making progress and fixes every mismatched vertices in the end.

The main idea is based on the following expansion property of random graphs.

Let S𝑆S with |S|=θ​n𝑆𝜃𝑛|S|=\theta n where θ≤1/100𝜃1100\theta\leq 1/100. In random graph G∼𝔾​(n,q)similar-to𝐺𝔾𝑛𝑞G\sim\mathbb{G}(n,q), we let random variable X=|E​(S,S)|𝑋𝐸𝑆𝑆X=|E(S,S)| and μ=𝔼​[X]=q​(θ​n2)𝜇𝔼delimited-[]𝑋𝑞binomial𝜃𝑛2\mu=\mathbb{E}[X]=q\binom{\theta n}{2}. By Chernoff’s bound, we know that the probability that X−μ>k​θ2​n2​q𝑋𝜇𝑘superscript𝜃2superscript𝑛2𝑞X-\mu>\sqrt{k\theta^{2}n^{2}q} is exponentially small in k𝑘k. Thus, as long as k𝑘k is larger than 10​log⁡(nθ​n)≤10​θ​n​log⁡n10binomial𝑛𝜃𝑛10𝜃𝑛𝑛10\log\binom{n}{\theta n}\leq 10\theta n\log n then we can simply take an union bound over all possible S𝑆S. As a result,X𝑋\displaystyle X≤μ+10​θ​n​log⁡n​θ2​n2​qabsent𝜇10𝜃𝑛𝑛superscript𝜃2superscript𝑛2𝑞\displaystyle\leq\mu+\sqrt{10\theta n\log n\theta^{2}n^{2}q}≤q​n​|S|20000+q​n​|S|⋅10​θ​log⁡nq​n≤|S|​Δ′100absent𝑞𝑛𝑆20000⋅𝑞𝑛𝑆10𝜃𝑛𝑞𝑛𝑆superscriptΔ′100\displaystyle\leq\frac{qn|S|}{20000}+qn|S|\cdot\sqrt{\frac{10\theta\log n}{qn}}\leq\frac{|S|\Delta^{\prime}}{100}with high probability.∎

Next, apply averaging argument on 3.28 and G𝐺G to be the intersection graph and S𝑆S to be the set of mismatched vertices, there are at least |S|/2𝑆2|S|/2 vertices in S𝑆S having less than Δ′/50superscriptΔ′50\Delta^{\prime}/50 edges to vertices in S𝑆S. Thus, Step 2: Fixing the solution would correct |S|/2𝑆2|S|/2 many vertices.

As for the case where δ=o​(p​γ)𝛿𝑜𝑝𝛾\delta=o(p\gamma), the size of mismatched vertices is o​(Δ′)𝑜superscriptΔ′o(\Delta^{\prime}) and thus Step 2: Fixing the solution would fix all the remaining vertices.∎

Here, we tie up loose ends and prove Theorem 3.2.

The proof of correctness follows together from Lemma 3.13 and from Lemma 3.21.

The running time of Algorithm 3.4 is nO​(log⁡n)superscript𝑛𝑂𝑛n^{O(\log n)}, since it simply requires constructing ℋℋ\mathcal{H} (which cannot take more than poly​((v2d′​v))=vO​(v)polybinomialsuperscript𝑣2superscript𝑑′𝑣superscript𝑣𝑂𝑣\text{poly}(\binom{v^{2}}{d^{\prime}v})=v^{O(v)} time), then looking for the subgraphs in ℋℋ\mathcal{H} in G1,G2subscript𝐺1subscript𝐺2G_{1},G_{2} (which takes (nv)​|ℋ|=vO​(v)⋅nO​(v)=nO​(v)binomial𝑛𝑣ℋ⋅superscript𝑣𝑂𝑣superscript𝑛𝑂𝑣superscript𝑛𝑂𝑣\binom{n}{v}|\mathcal{H}|=v^{O(v)}\cdot n^{O(v)}=n^{O(v)} time). The runtime of Algorithm 3.22 is polynomial time. Thus, we conclude that there is a quasi-polynomial time algorithm that solves Problem 3.1 with high probability in the specified parameter region.∎

Both the distinguishing and recovery algorithms rely on a carefully chosen “test set” of graphs with a set of delicate properties.In this section, we construct test sets with these characteristics.

In fact, Theorem 4.1 is the union of Propositions 4.2, 4.9 and 4.16, each of which covers a different set of densities d𝑑d (and each of which contains a more precise quantitative statement).

When d𝑑d is an integer, random d𝑑d-regular graph satisfy all but condition 4, and the graphs have sufficiently different edge sets on average that we can argue that a sufficiently large fraction of d𝑑d-regular graphs can be chosen while still satisfying condition 5.

On the other hand, when d𝑑d is a non-integer, the strict balancedness property is less straightforward to satisfy.Further, for recovery we require a strengthened, quantitative notion of strict balance.In Section 4.1, we will prove that random d𝑑d-regular graphs satisfy our requirements, and in Sections 4.2.1 and 4.2.2 we design constructions that leverage d𝑑d-regular regular random graphs to obtain graphs of dense and sparse non-integer average degrees.

Suppose that we were only interested in constructing test sets of graphs with integer average degree d≥3𝑑3d\geq 3.For those cases, random d𝑑d-regular graphs will satisfy almost all of our requirements for the individual graphs H𝐻H, and we will prove that there is a large subset of graphs on d𝑑d vertices so that every pair of graphs have no dense common subgraphs.

Random d𝑑d-regular graphs are known to satisfy many of our requirements, either absolutely or with high probability.For example, the following well-known fact:

If H𝐻H is a d𝑑d-regular graph and J𝐽J is a proper subgraph of H𝐻H, then J𝐽J must have at least one vertex of degree less than d𝑑d.∎

Additionally, classical results assert that random d𝑑d-regular graphs are with high probability connected, and also contain no non-trivial automorphisms.

It is not too difficult to see that there are at least vΩ​(v)superscript𝑣Ω𝑣v^{\Omega(v)} d𝑑d-regular graphs on v𝑣v vertices; however, a precise formula is also known.The following is a consequence of a theorem of McKay and Wormwald.

Now, we will prove that there is a set of at least vΩ​(d​v)superscript𝑣Ω𝑑𝑣v^{\Omega(dv)} d𝑑d-regular graphs that have all of the above properties, while not sharing any dense subgraphs.

We construct a meta-graph G𝐺G whose vertices correspond to d𝑑d-regular graphs.For two d𝑑d-regular graphs H,H′𝐻superscript𝐻′H,H^{\prime}, we add an edge between their vertices in G𝐺G if H𝐻H and H′superscript𝐻′H^{\prime} intersect on a subgraph J𝐽J of average degree at least α⋅d⋅𝛼𝑑\alpha\cdot d.

First, we argue that most H𝐻H do not have small subgraphs of density α𝛼\alpha.This follows from a result of Bollobás, who proves that d𝑑d-regular random graphs are good expanders.

For constant-sized subsets, we also use the following known lemma.

So to rule out dense shared subgraphs on fewer than 100100100 vertices, we use Lemma 4.8, to conclude that all but an o​(1)𝑜1o(1) fraction of d𝑑d-regular graphs cannot contain a subgraph on at most 100100100 with density >1absent1>1.

For larger subgraphs, we apply Theorem 4.7, concluding that only a o​(1)𝑜1o(1) fraction of all d𝑑d-regular graphs contain subgraphs J𝐽J of size less than v/2𝑣2v/2 with more than (d​|V​(J)|−350​d​|V​(J)|)/2=d​47100⋅|V​(J)|𝑑𝑉𝐽350𝑑𝑉𝐽2⋅𝑑47100𝑉𝐽(d|V(J)|-\tfrac{3}{50}d|V(J)|)/2=d\tfrac{47}{100}\cdot|V(J)| edges.Thus, for a “typical” pair of d𝑑d-regular graphs H,H′𝐻superscript𝐻′H,H^{\prime} and for any J⊂H,H′𝐽𝐻superscript𝐻′J\subset H,H^{\prime} with |V​(J)|∈[100,v/2]𝑉𝐽100𝑣2|V(J)|\in[100,v/2] the conclusion holds:|E​(J)|<d​47100​|V​(J)|≤d​48100​|V​(J)|−d,𝐸𝐽𝑑47100𝑉𝐽𝑑48100𝑉𝐽𝑑|E(J)|<d\tfrac{47}{100}|V(J)|\leq d\tfrac{48}{100}|V(J)|-d,where the final inequality holds by our lower bound |V​(J)|≥101𝑉𝐽101|V(J)|\geq 101.

Now, we will use our integer-average-degree sets to construct a test set for graphs of non-integer average degrees.

Here, we will show that for graphs of average degree at least 666.In this regime, the expansion properties of d𝑑d-regular random graphs are so strong that they allow us to tread less delicately, and simply add a random matching on top of a d𝑑d-regular random graph to obtain a graph with average degree between d𝑑d and d+1𝑑1d+1, without jeopardizing the strict balancedness property.

Our construction will be as follows:

Our construction will be as follows: we sample a d𝑑d-regular random graph H𝐻H, we add a random matching M𝑀M on λ⋅v⋅𝜆𝑣\lambda\cdot v random vertices to obtain the graph H∪M𝐻𝑀H\cup M, and we add it to the set 𝒢𝒢\mathcal{G} if H∪M𝐻𝑀H\cup M is simple and if H∈ℋdv𝐻superscriptsubscriptℋ𝑑𝑣H\in\mathcal{H}_{d}^{v}.Then, we will find the largest subset of pairs (H,M)∈ℋ𝐻𝑀ℋ(H,M)\in\mathcal{H} such that no pair of graphs H∪M𝐻𝑀H\cup M and H′∪M′superscript𝐻′superscript𝑀′H^{\prime}\cup M^{\prime} overlap on a subgraph with average degree larger than d−15𝑑15d-\tfrac{1}{5}.

First, we prove that H∪M𝐻𝑀H\cup M is simple with constant probability.

The probability that H∪M𝐻𝑀H\cup M is simple is at least the probability that a random (d+1)𝑑1(d+1)-regular graph drawn from the configuration model is simple; this is known to occur with probability at least exp⁡(1−(d+1)24)1superscript𝑑124\exp(\frac{1-(d+1)^{2}}{4}) [Wor].∎

Each H∪M𝐻𝑀H\cup M clearly has the desired average degree; we will prove that our other desired properties hold as well.

Applying Theorem 4.7 with cdsubscript𝑐𝑑c_{d} the expansion constant of d𝑑d-regular random graphs, we have that in H𝐻H, for every subgraph S𝑆S containing at most v/2𝑣2v/2 vertices, S𝑆S contains at most (1−cd)​d2​|S|1subscript𝑐𝑑𝑑2𝑆(1-c_{d})\frac{d}{2}|S| edges.

To establish our claim, we utilize the following result which is a corollary of a theorem of McKay and Wormwald.

Standard arguments (see e.g. [JLR11]) establish that our distribution over graphs is contiguous with the uniform distribution over simple graphs on v𝑣v vertices with this degree sequence, therefore this conclusion holds for the set 𝒢𝒢\mathcal{G} as well.∎

In this subsection, we describe a graph family ℋℋ\operatorname{\mathcal{H}} of graphs of density close to 1 that satisfies the desired properties.In this regime, our strategy is to sample a 333-regular random graph and subdivide its edges into paths.If we want to achieve an arbitrary average degree, the paths that we subdivide cannot all have the same length.However, when we subdivide into paths that are sufficiently long, the expansion of the 333-regular graph will compensate for the asymmetry.181818Of course, one might hope that choosing the lengths of the edges randomly will also compensate for the asymmetry, regardless of the path lengths; we conjecture that this is the case but have not proven it as of yet.Using this intuition, we are able to prove that our construction satisfies the desired properties when our target density is close to 1 (few degree-3 vertices relative to the number of degree-2 vertices).

In this subsection, we design test sets with density close to 1.

Let H∈ℋ′𝐻superscriptℋ′H\in\mathcal{H}^{\prime}, and let H3subscript𝐻3H_{3} be the corresponding 333-regular graph (given by shrinking every k𝑘k– or (k+1)𝑘1(k+1)-path to a single edge).H′superscript𝐻′H^{\prime} has average degree d′superscript𝑑′d^{\prime} by construction.

This follows from the fact that H3subscript𝐻3H_{3} has no non-trivial automorphisms; an automorphism of H𝐻H corresponds to an automorphism of H3subscript𝐻3H_{3}, as in an automorphism of H𝐻H, every subdivided edge is mapped to a subdivided edge.∎

The proof of Lemma 4.18 is somewhat involved, and we prove it below after completing the proof of the proposition.

Finally, it remains to prove that many pairs H,H′∈ℋ𝐻superscript𝐻′ℋH,H^{\prime}\in\mathcal{H} do not overlap on dense subgraphs.

First, we use Lemma 4.8 (and our construction of ℋ3subscriptℋ3\mathcal{H}_{3}) to conclude that for any shared subgraph J𝐽J of H,H′𝐻superscript𝐻′H,H^{\prime} with at most 100100100 vertices in H3subscript𝐻3H_{3} or H3′subscriptsuperscript𝐻′3H^{\prime}_{3}, the density is bounded by 111.

Since H3,H3′subscript𝐻3subscriptsuperscript𝐻′3H_{3},H^{\prime}_{3} come from ℋ3λ​vsuperscriptsubscriptℋ3𝜆𝑣\mathcal{H}_{3}^{\lambda v}, we already have that H3,H3′subscript𝐻3subscriptsuperscript𝐻′3H_{3},H^{\prime}_{3} do not overlap on any subgraph J3subscript𝐽3J_{3} with |E​(J3)|≥1225⋅3​|V​(J3)|−3𝐸subscript𝐽3⋅12253𝑉subscript𝐽33|E(J_{3})|\geq\frac{12}{25}\cdot 3|V(J_{3})|-3.If H3,H3′subscript𝐻3subscriptsuperscript𝐻′3H_{3},H^{\prime}_{3} overlap on a subgraph J3subscript𝐽3J_{3} of size at least 101101101 with average degree δ𝛿\delta, then in H,H′𝐻superscript𝐻′H,H^{\prime} the corresponding subgraph J𝐽J will have density at most|E​(J)||V​(J)|≤12​δ​|V​(J3)|⋅(k+1)|V​(J3)|+12​δ​|V​(J3)|⋅k≤1+12​δ−11+12​δ⋅k,𝐸𝐽𝑉𝐽⋅12𝛿𝑉subscript𝐽3𝑘1𝑉subscript𝐽3⋅12𝛿𝑉subscript𝐽3𝑘112𝛿11⋅12𝛿𝑘\frac{|E(J)|}{|V(J)|}\leq\frac{\tfrac{1}{2}\delta|V(J_{3})|\cdot(k+1)}{|V(J_{3})|+\tfrac{1}{2}\delta|V(J_{3})|\cdot k}\leq 1+\frac{\tfrac{1}{2}\delta-1}{1+\tfrac{1}{2}\delta\cdot k},and again if we use that k>3​(1−λ)2​λ−1𝑘31𝜆2𝜆1k>\tfrac{3(1-\lambda)}{2\lambda}-1,and that δ<3−150𝛿3150\delta<3-\tfrac{1}{50} we get that|E​(J)||V​(J)|≤1+12​δ−11+12​δ⋅(3​(1−λ)2​λ−1)<1+λ4,𝐸𝐽𝑉𝐽112𝛿11⋅12𝛿31𝜆2𝜆11𝜆4\frac{|E(J)|}{|V(J)|}\leq 1+\frac{\tfrac{1}{2}\delta-1}{1+\tfrac{1}{2}\delta\cdot(\tfrac{3(1-\lambda)}{2\lambda}-1)}<1+\frac{\lambda}{4},whenever λ<11109𝜆11109\lambda<\frac{11}{109}.Since we have assumed that |V​(J3)|≥100𝑉subscript𝐽3100|V(J_{3})|\geq 100 and that the density of J3subscript𝐽3J_{3} is larger than 111, |V​(J)|≥100⋅k≥100​(3​(1−λ)λ−1)>6λ𝑉𝐽⋅100𝑘10031𝜆𝜆16𝜆|V(J)|\geq 100\cdot k\geq 100(\tfrac{3(1-\lambda)}{\lambda}-1)>\frac{6}{\lambda}, and we have that|E​(J)|​<(1+λ3)|​V​(J)|−2,conditional𝐸𝐽bra1𝜆3𝑉𝐽2|E(J)|<(1+\frac{\lambda}{3})|V(J)|-2,as desired.∎

Finally, we note that the size of ℋd′vsuperscriptsubscriptℋsuperscript𝑑′𝑣\mathcal{H}_{d^{\prime}}^{v} is simply the size of ℋ3λ​vsuperscriptsubscriptℋ3𝜆𝑣\mathcal{H}_{3}^{\lambda v}, from which we get our bound.∎

We now prove that our graphs are strictly balanced; in fact we will need a quantitative version of this statement, which first requires the following supporting lemma.

First, the following claim allows us to consider only J𝐽J corresponding to full edge-induced J3⊂Hsubscript𝐽3𝐻J_{3}\subset H.

The edge e𝑒e is subdivided into a path.If J𝐽J contains a strict sub-path, then it must contain a vertex of degree 111.In a graph of density at least 111, density can only increase when vertices of degree-111 are removed.Therefore, if we remove the degree-1 vertices one-by-one until we obtain J~~𝐽\tilde{J}, we obtain a graph that can only be denser.∎

By Claim 4.21, J𝐽J is at its densest if every subdivided edge from H𝐻H is either present or completely missing, thus we may restrict our attention to subgraphs J𝐽J which correspond exactly to edge-induced subgraphs J3subscript𝐽3J_{3} of H3subscript𝐻3H_{3}, with all subdivided edges fully present.

Finally, we prove Lemma 4.18.See 4.18

Here, we consider arbitrary edge-induced subgraphs J⊆H𝐽𝐻J\subseteq H.We let J3⊆H3subscript𝐽3subscript𝐻3J_{3}\subseteq H_{3} be the edge-induced subgraph of H3subscript𝐻3H_{3} which contains any edge for which the corresponding path in J𝐽J is at least partially present.That is, we allow the subdivided path corresponding to each edge ℓ∈E​(J3)ℓ𝐸subscript𝐽3\ell\in E(J_{3}) to be only partially present in J𝐽J.For an edge ℓ∈E​(J3)ℓ𝐸subscript𝐽3\ell\in E(J_{3}), we’ll let and VJ​(ℓ),EJ​(ℓ)subscript𝑉𝐽ℓsubscript𝐸𝐽ℓV_{J}(\ell),E_{J}(\ell) be the vertex set and edge set of the corresponding path in J𝐽J, and define VH​(ℓ),EH​(ℓ)subscript𝑉𝐻ℓsubscript𝐸𝐻ℓV_{H}(\ell),E_{H}(\ell) correspondingly for H𝐻H; also define k​(ℓ)𝑘ℓk(\ell) to be the path length of the path corresponding to ℓℓ\ell in H𝐻H.
